Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
10 min
Coercion and the Validity of Verbal Dispositions and Contracts
Explains effects of coercion on various verbal acts and contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify verbal acts under coercion into those that remain valid (e.g., divorce) and those that become corrupt but can be ratified (e.g., sale), and explain why confessions are invalid.

Core Idea
Coercion does not nullify irrevocable verbal acts like divorce, but renders revocable contracts corrupt and confessions void.

This lesson covers the second major part of the passage, which delves into how coercion affects various verbal acts and contracts. The author distinguishes between acts that are not revocable and do not require consent (like divorce) which remain effective under coercion, and those that are revocable and require consent (like sale) which become corrupt but may be validated later. Confessions under coercion are entirely invalid because the element of truth is absent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of verbal acts
Acts that are not revocable (e.g., divorce) vs acts that are revocable and require consent (e.g., sale).
2
Apply the rule to non-revocable acts
Note that coercion does not nullify these acts; they are executed on the coerced person.
3
Apply the rule to revocable acts
Understand that such acts become corrupt (fasid) but can be ratified later.
4
Analyze confessions under coercion
Recognize that they are invalid because the basis of confession (truth) is negated.
5
Consider the analogy to jest (hazl)
Understand why coercion is not analogous to jest; in coercion, the agent does not consent to the cause.

(القمر) والتدبير : هو أن يقول لعبده مثلاً: إن مت فأنت حر، والظهار: تشبيه زوجته أو ما عبّر به عنها أو جزء شائع منها بعضو يحرم نظره إليه من أعضاء محارمه نسبًا أو رضاعا، والإيلاء: حلف يمنع وطء الزوجة مدة الإيلاء، وهي للحرة أربعة أشهر وللأمة شهران، والفيء: هو الرجوع عن
الإيلاء الذي هو اليمين والفيء القولي: هو أن يقول مثلاً: فئتُ إليها، كذا في "الوقاية" وغيرها.(القمر) فينعقد البيع فاسدًا أما الانعقاد فلصدورها من أهلها في محلها، وأما الفساد فلفوات الرضاء الذي هو شرط النفاذ حتى لو أجاز المكره بعد زوال الإكراه يصح لزوال المفسد (السنبلي)
كلها: أي سواء كانت بما يحتمل الفسخ أو بما لا يحتمله وسواء كانت بالإكراه الملجئ أو لغيره. (القمر)
Statement of legal capacity. Statement of coercion and its divisions. When one is coerced into it, breaking the fast becomes permissible for him, and in some of it, acting upon it is a concession, like uttering the word of disbelief on his tongue if he is coerced into it; that is permitted for him on condition that the heart is at peace with faith. Coercion, i.e., uttering the word of disbelief, is overwhelming. The difference between permissibility (ibaha) and concession (rukhsa) is that in concession, that act is not made permissible such that its prohibition is removed, but it is treated like the permissible in removing sin, whereas in permissibility, the prohibition is removed. It was said: There is no need to mention permissibility because it is included in obligation or concession; for if what is meant by it is the permissibility of the act with sin—i.e., with permissibility—in endurance, then it is obligation; and if without sin in endurance, then it is concession. So the breaking of fast by a coerced fasting person: if he is a traveler, it is obligatory; if he is a resident, it is a concession; and there is no act where initiation and abstention are equal in sin and reward so that it would be permissible. Coercion does not contradict choice, i.e., coercion does not negate the choice of the coerced person (maf'ul bih), but the choice is defective (fasid). So when—i.e., the choice of the coerced—it is opposed by a sound choice, which is the choice of the coercer (mukrih), it is necessary to give precedence to the sound over the defective if possible, as in coercion to kill and destroy property, where the coerced person can be made an instrument for the coercer, so the act is attributed to the coercer and its ruling applies to him. Otherwise—i.e., if the attribution of the act to the coercer is not possible, as in statements and some acts—it remains attributed to the defective choice, which is the choice of the coerced person, so the coerced is held accountable for his act, like eating and drinking. Then he branched off from this with his saying: 'So in statements, the coerced person cannot be an instrument for another because speaking with the tongue of another is inconceivable, so the ruling of the statement is confined to the coerced person—i.e., the prohibition of that act. (al-Qamar) Preferring the sound: sound choice is when the agent acts with independent intention and choice; defective choice is when the agent acts for another if attribution of the act to the coercer is possible. (al-Qamar) The act, i.e., killing and destroying property. (al-Qamar) And he confined it (the ruling) to the coerced person. Bahar al-Ulum, our master Abd al-Ali said: 'Speaking with the tongue of another is impossible, but it does not necessitate confining (the ruling) to the direct agent, the coerced person; rather, what is closer to reason is that such a statement is nullified.' If the statement is of a kind that is not revocable and does not depend on consent, it is not nullified by coercion, like divorce and its like from emancipation, marriage, return (ruj'a), tadbir, pardon for intentional homicide, oath, vow, zihar, ila', and verbal return (fi') in it, and Islam. For all these dispositions are not susceptible to dissolution and do not depend on consent. So if someone is coerced into them and utters them, they are not nullified by coercion and are executed only upon the coerced person. And if it is of a kind that is revocable and depends on consent, like sale and its like, then it is confined to the direct agent here as well—i.e., the dissolution—the coerced person. Like leasing, except that it becomes corrupt due to lack of consent, so the sale is concluded as corrupt; if he ratifies it after the coercion ceases, it becomes valid because the corrupting factor is removed by ratification. All confessions (iqrar) are not valid because their validity depends on the existence of the thing confessed to, and its indication points to its non-existence—i.e., the non-establishment of the thing confessed—because he speaks to ward off the sword from himself, not due to the existence of the thing confessed. And it is not permissible to treat it as a metaphor for something else because he does not intend metaphor when evidence of falsehood (coercion) exists. As for acts (af'al), there are two types: one type is like statements, so the coerced person cannot be an instrument for another, like eating—i.e., confession—and its ruling is not established because it was issued under coercion, and its analogy to jest (hazl) is invalid because the jester is satisfied with initiating the cause even if he is not satisfied with the ruling. But in our case, the coerced person is not satisfied with the cause; rather, he performs it under coercion, so it is void. Reflect. (al-Qamar) 'And it does not depend' etc. such that it occurs also by jest. (al-Qamar) Tadbir: that one says to his slave, for example: 'If I die, you are free.' Zihar: likening his wife or what is expressed by her or a part of her to a body part whose sight is prohibited from his relatives by blood or marriage. Ila': an oath preventing intercourse with the wife for the duration of ila', which for a free woman is four months and for a slave woman two months. Fi': returning from ila', which is the oath; and verbal fi' is that one says, for example, 'I have returned to her,' as in al-Wiqaya and others. (al-Qamar) 'So the sale is concluded as corrupt'—its conclusion is because it issues from its proper agent in its proper place; its corruptness is due to the lack of consent, which is a condition of execution, such that if the coerced person ratifies after the coercion ceases, it becomes valid due to the removal of the corrupting factor. (al-Sanbali) 'All'—i.e., whether they are of the kind that admits dissolution or not, and whether under overwhelming coercion or other. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage from Noor al-Anwar deals with the usul al-fiqh topic of legal capacity (ahliyya) and specifically the impediment of coercion (ikrah) and its effects on various actions. The commentary expands upon the base text, distinguishing between permissibility (ibaha) and concession (rukhsa) in the context of coercion. It clarifies that while coercion permits certain acts (like breaking the fast for a traveler or uttering disbelief if heart is firm in faith), these are concessions that remove sin without necessarily making the act intrinsically permissible. The text then explains that coercion does not negate choice entirely; the coerced person still has a defective choice (ikhtiyar fasid), but when a sound choice (the coercer's) opposes it, the sound choice is given precedence if the coerced can be considered a mere instrument (as in killing or destroying property). However, for statements, the coerced cannot be an instrument, so the ruling applies to him directly. The passage lists actions that are not nullified by coercion (divorce, emancipation, etc.) because they are not revocable and do not require consent. In contrast, contracts like sale that require consent are concluded corruptly (fasid) but can be ratified later. Confessions under coercion are entirely invalid because the very basis of confession—truthfulness—is negated. Finally, physical acts like eating under coercion are attributed to the coerced person, and the attempt to analogize them to jest fails because the coerced lacks the intent behind the act. Throughout, the commentary by al-Qamar and others provides glosses and subtle refinements.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrāh): Compelling a person to do something against his will, typically by threat of harm.
الاختيار الفاسد (al-ikhtiyār al-fāsid): Defective choice that occurs under coercion; it is a choice but not a sound, independent one.
الإقرار (al-iqrār): Confession or admission of a right or fact, typically requiring truthfulness for validity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under coercion, which of the following becomes a corrupt (fasid) contract that can be ratified?
A) Divorce
B) Sale
C) Emancipation
D) Oath

Key Takeaway
Coercion leaves irrevocable verbal acts intact but corrupts revocable contracts; confessions are entirely void because the element of truth is missing.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Irrevocable Utterances Not Affected by Coercion
Ruling statement on coercion and irrevocable utterances

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which utterances are not invalidated by coercion according to the Hanafi school and explain the legal reasoning.

Core Idea
Coercion does not invalidate irrevocable utterances like divorce because they do not depend on consent.

Coercion (ikrah) is a factor that can affect the validity of legal acts. However, not all acts are vulnerable to it. The Hanafi school distinguishes between utterances that are inherently irrevocable and those that depend on consent. This lesson focuses on the first category: acts like divorce, manumission, and marriage, which remain valid even under duress. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the rules of coercion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of utterance
Determine if the utterance is of the kind that cannot be dissolved (la yanfasikh) and does not depend on consent.
The text explicitly classifies these as 'tasarrufat' that do not admit dissolution.
2
Locate the examples
List the examples given: divorce, manumission, marriage, revocation, tadbir, pardon, oath, vow, zihar, ila, verbal return, and Islam.
These are all unilateral acts or circumstances that create immediate legal effects.
3
Apply the rule
Conclude that such utterances are valid despite coercion because they are not dependent on consent.
The text states: 'لا تحتمل الفسخ ولا تتوقف على الرضاء'.
4
Note the practical consequence
The coerced person cannot later claim invalidity; the act takes effect immediately.

(السنبلي) فإن كان أي المكره الأكل جائعًا. (القمر) فيجب عليه الحد :قلت وقال في بعض شروح "الحسامي": لا يجب به الحدّ على واحد منهما، ويجب به العقر على المحمول، ولا يرجع به على الحامل؛ لأن منفعة الوطء حصلت له، والله تعالى أعلم. (السنبلي)
Statement of legal capacity. Explanation of coercion and its divisions. If the statement is of the kind that cannot be dissolved and does not depend on consent, it is not invalidated by coercion, such as divorce and similar matters from manumission, marriage, revocation, tadbir, pardon for intentional homicide, oath, vow, zihar, ila, verbal return (fi'), and Islam. Indeed all these transactions cannot be dissolved and do not depend on consent. So if one is coerced into them and utters them, it is not invalidated by coercion, and they take effect upon the coerced (with fatha) only. And if it can be dissolved and depends on consent, like sale and the like, then it is confined to the direct agent here as well, which is dissolution like hire, except that it becomes corrupt due to lack of consent; so the sale is concluded as corrupt, and if the coerced ratifies it after coercion ceases, it becomes valid because the corrupting factor is removed by ratification. And all admissions are not valid because their validity depends on the existence of what is admitted; the indication of it [the admission] has demonstrated its absence, i.e., the non-existence of what is admitted, because he speaks to repel the sword from himself, not due to the existence of what is admitted. It is not permissible to make it a metaphor for something because he does not intend the metaphor while there is evidence of falsehood, which is coercion. And actions are of two types: one type is like words, so it is not appropriate for the coerced person to be a tool for another, such as eating... (interpretation: i.e., the admission) and its ruling is not established because it was issued under coercion. Analogy with jest is not valid, for the jester is pleased with effecting the cause, even if he is not pleased with the ruling; whereas in our case, the coerced is not pleased with the cause, rather he effects it under coercion, so it becomes null. Consider. (Al-Qamar) 'And does not depend' etc. such that it occurs by jest as well. (Al-Qamar) At-Tadbir: he says to his slave, for example: if I die, you are free. Az-Zihar: likening his wife or what is expressed by her or a common part of her to a body part to which it is unlawful for him to look from among his mahram relatives by lineage or suckling. Al-Ila: an oath preventing intercourse with the wife for the period of ila, which for a free woman is four months and for a slave woman two months. Al-Fay': returning from ila, which is the oath; verbal fay': he says, for example: I have returned to her. As in Al-Wiqayah and others. (Al-Qamar) The sale is concluded as corrupt. As for its conclusion, it is because it issues from its people in its place. As for its corruption, it is due to the loss of consent, which is a condition for validity. So much so that if the coerced ratifies after coercion ceases, it becomes valid due to the removal of the corrupting factor. (As-Sunbuli) 'All of them': i.e., whether they are of the type that can be dissolved or not, and whether by overwhelming coercion or otherwise. (Al-Qamar) And sexual intercourse, and fornication, are confined to the coerced person because eating with another's mouth is inconceivable, and likewise intercourse with another's organ is inconceivable. So if a person is coerced into eating during a fast, the fast of the eater is broken and the fast of the commander is not broken if he is fasting. Similarly, if he is coerced into eating another's property, the eater sins and the commander does not. However, they differed regarding liability: it is said that liability rests on the coerced, not the commander, even if the coerced is a tool for the commander in terms of destruction, because the benefit of eating accrues to him. And it is said: if he is coerced into eating his own property, if he is hungry, nothing is owed by the commander because his benefit returns to the eater; if he is full, its value is owed because his benefit did not return to the eater. And if he is coerced into eating another's property, liability rests on the coerced, whether he is hungry or full, because it is a kind of coercion to destroy his property, so liability is due. Likewise, if a person is coerced into intercourse, if it is with a woman other than his wife, the hadd punishment is upon him, and he suffers pain, and this act does not transfer to the commander according to what will follow. And if it is with his wife during a fast, i'tikaf, ihram, or menses, then this too should be confined to the agent, and he sins, and he must undertake what is required of making up and expiation, and liability upon his property, and I have not seen a narration that he may claim recourse against the coercer commander or not. (As-Sunbuli) 'If he is hungry' meaning the coerced eater. (Al-Qamar) 'Then the hadd is upon him': I said, and in some commentaries of Al-Husami: the hadd is not necessary upon either of them, but the 'aqr (dower equivalent) is due upon the one on whom intercourse is committed, and he does not have recourse against the one who compelled him because the benefit of intercourse accrued to him, and Allah knows best. (As-Sunbuli)
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage, from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh work, explains the effect of coercion (ikrah) on utterances and actions. It first distinguishes between utterances that cannot be dissolved and do not depend on consent — such as divorce, manumission, marriage, etc. — which remain valid despite coercion, because the speaker is considered a direct agent and coercion does not vitiate the legal cause. Conversely, utterances that can be dissolved and depend on consent, like sale, become corrupt (fasid) under coercion but are validated if the coerced person ratifies after coercion ends. Admissions (iqrar) are entirely invalid under coercion because they are presumptively false — the coerced person speaks only to avoid harm, not to affirm reality. The text then turns to actions, dividing them into two types: those like words (where the coerced cannot be a mere tool) and others. For physical acts like eating and intercourse, the coerced person is always the direct agent, so fasting may be broken, sin may be incurred, and liability for damages falls on him. The commentary (by al-Qamar, al-Sunbuli) nuances these rules, discussing disagreements about liability and whether the commander can be held responsible. The final principle states that all actions and words of the coerced person are considered valid because they issue from intellect and legal capacity, unless a changing factor (mughtayyar) like an exception or condition alters the ruling. This passage is typical of the Hanafi method of analyzing legal capacity (ahliyyah) and its impediments.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrāh): Compelling a person to do something against his will, either by threat of harm (mulji') or less.
الفسخ (al-faskh): Dissolution of a contract; here used to denote contracts that can be annulled.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in the text, what is the effect of coercion on divorce?
A) The divorce is invalid and has no effect.
B) The divorce is valid and takes effect.
C) The divorce is suspended until the coerced person ratifies it.
D) The divorce is considered null unless pronounced orally.

Key Takeaway
Coercion does not affect the validity of irrevocable utterances like divorce in Hanafi fiqh.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate

Contracts Under Coercion: Sale and Ratification
Ruling statement on contracts under coercion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a sale under coercion is considered fasid (corrupt) and how ratification restores validity.

Core Idea
A coerced sale is fasid but can become valid through ratification, because consent is a condition for validity.

Unlike irrevocable utterances, contracts like sale depend on consent for their validity. Coercion negates consent, rendering the contract corrupt (fasid). However, Hanafi fiqh provides a remedy: if the coerced party ratifies the contract after the coercion ends, it becomes valid. This lesson explores the reasoning behind this ruling and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nature of the contract
Note that contracts like sale can be dissolved (yahtamilu al-faskh) and depend on consent (yatawaqqaf 'ala al-rida).
2
Determine the effect of coercion
The contract is concluded but fasid (corrupt) due to lack of consent.
The text says 'ينعقد البيع فاسدًا' – it is a concluded but defective contract.
3
Consider ratification
If the coerced person ratifies (ijazah) after coercion ceases, the contract becomes valid because the corrupting factor is removed.
'ولو أجازه بعد زوال الإكراه يصح؛ لأن المفسد زال بالإجازة'.
4
Explain the underlying principle
Consent is a condition for validity; coercion negates it, but subsequent ratification supplies consent.

Source Text

بيان الأهلية
بيان الإكراه وأقسامه
فإن كان القول مما لا ينفسخ ولا يتوقف على الرضاء لم يبطل بالكره كالطلاق ونحوه من العتاق، والنكاح والرجعة والتدبير، والعفو عن دم العمد، واليمين، والنذر، والظهار، والإيلاء، والفيء القولي ،فيه والإسلام فإن هذه التصرفات كلها لا تحتمل الفسخ ولا تتوقف على الرضاء، فلو أكره بها أحد وتكلّم بها لم يبطل بالكُره، وتنفذ على المكره بالفتح فقط.
وإن كان يحتمله ويتوقف على الرضاء كالبيع ونحوه يقتصر على المباشر ههنا أيضا، وهو الفسخ كالإجارة إلا أنه يفسد لعدم الرضاء، فينعقد البيع فاسدًا، ولو أجازه بعد زوال الإكراه يصح؛ لأن المفسد زال بالإجازة.
ولا تصح الأقارير كلها؛ لأن صحتها تعتمد على قيام المخبر بها، وقد قامت دلالتها على عدمه، أي عدم ثبوت المخبر بها؛ لأنه يتكلّم دفعًا للسيف عن نفسه، لا بوجود المخبر بها، ولا يجوز أن يجعل مجازا عن شيء؛ لأنه لا يقصد المجاز مع قيام دليل الكذب، وهو الإكراه. والأفعال قسمان: أحدهما كالأقوال، فلا يصلح أن يكون المكره فيه آلة لغيره كالأكل،
=
أي الإقرار
ولا يثبت حكمه ؛ لأنه صدر بالإكراه، وقياسه على الهزل لا يصح، فإن الهازل راض بإيقاع السبب، وإن كان لا يرضى بالحكم، وأما فيما نحن فيه فالمكره لا يرضى بالسبب، بل يوقعه بالإكراه فيبطل، فتأمل (القمر) ولا يتوقف إلخ بحيث يقع بالهزل أيضًا. (القمر) والتدبير : هو أن يقول لعبده مثلاً: إن مت فأنت حر، والظهار: تشبيه زوجته أو ما عبّر به عنها أو جزء شائع منها بعضو يحرم نظره إليه من أعضاء محارمه نسبًا أو رضاعا، والإيلاء: حلف يمنع وطء الزوجة مدة الإيلاء، وهي للحرة أربعة أشهر وللأمة شهران، والفيء: هو الرجوع عن الإيلاء الذي هو اليمين والفيء القولي: هو أن يقول مثلاً: فئتُ إليها، كذا في "الوقاية" وغيرها.(القمر) فينعقد البيع فاسدًا أما الانعقاد فلصدورها من أهلها في محلها، وأما الفساد فلفوات الرضاء الذي هو شرط النفاذ حتى لو أجاز المكره بعد زوال الإكراه يصح لزوال المفسد (السنبلي)
كلها: أي سواء كانت بما يحتمل الفسخ أو بما لا يحتمله وسواء كانت بالإكراه الملجئ أو لغيره. (القمر)

والوطء، والزنا، فيقتصر على المكره؛ لأن الأكل بفم الغير لا يتصوّر، وكذا الوطء بآلة الغير لا يتصوّر ، فإذا أكره الإنسان أن يأكل في الصوم يفسد صوم الأكل ولا يفسد صوم الأمر إن كان صائمًا، وكذا لو أكره أن يأكل مال غيره يأثم الأكل دون الأمر،
أي الأمر
ولكنهم اختلفوا في حق الضمان فقيل: يجب الضمان على المكره دون الآمر، وإن كان المكره يصلح آلة للآمر من حيث الإتلاف؛ لأن منفعة الأكل حصلت له، وقيل: لو أكره
أي للمكره
على أكل مال نفسه، فإن كان جائعا لا يجب على الآمر شيء؛ لأن منفعته رجعت إلى الأكل، وإن كان شبعان تجب عليه قيمته؛ لأن منفعته لم ترجعا إلى الآكل، ولو أكره
الأمر
أي الأكل
من
على أكل مال الغير يجب الضمان على المكره، سواء كان جائعا أو شبعان؛ لأنه قبيل الإكراه على إتلاف ماله فيجب الضمان وكذا إذا أكره إنسان أن يطأ، فإن كان
مع غير امرأته، فيجب عليه الحد ويكون آلما، ولا ينتقل هذا الفعل إلى الأمر على ما
الواطي
سيأتي، وإن كان مع امرأته في الصوم، أو في الاعتكاف أو الإحرام، أو الحيض، فينبغي أن يكون هذا أيضًا مقتصراً على الفاعل، ويأثم هو، ويجب ما يجب من القضاء والكفارة،
أي الواطي
والضمان في ماله وما رأيت رواية على أنه يرجع به على المكره الآمر أم لا.
الواطي
بالضمان
على المكره : إلا إذا غيّره دليل مثل فعل الطائع أي كما أن فعل الطائع وقوله لا يبطل، بل يعتبر إلا إذا لحقه مغيّر من استثناء أو تعليق، فحينئذ لا يعتبر كما إذا قال لامرأته: أنت طالق" يقع الطلاق بعد التكلّم، إلا إذا لحقه دليل مغيّر فحينئذٍ لا يقع كالاستثناء والتعليق وكذا إذا شرب الخمر أو زنى يعتبر ذلك، ويقع عليه الحد، إلا إذا لحقه مانع ومغيّر كتحقق تلك الأفعال في دار الحرب أو تمكين الشبهة فيها، فحينئذ لا يعتبر، فكذلك جمع أفعال المكره وأقواله تعتبر وتصح لصدورها عن عقل وأهلية ،خطاب إلا عند وجود المغير، فحينئذ لا تصح ولا تعتبر . (السنبلي) فإن كان أي المكره الأكل جائعًا. (القمر) فيجب عليه الحد :قلت وقال في بعض شروح "الحسامي": لا يجب به الحدّ على واحد منهما، ويجب به العقر على المحمول، ولا يرجع به على الحامل؛ لأن منفعة الوطء حصلت له، والله تعالى أعلم. (السنبلي)
Statement of legal capacity. Explanation of coercion and its divisions. If the statement is of the kind that cannot be dissolved and does not depend on consent, it is not invalidated by coercion, such as divorce and similar matters from manumission, marriage, revocation, tadbir, pardon for intentional homicide, oath, vow, zihar, ila, verbal return (fi'), and Islam. Indeed all these transactions cannot be dissolved and do not depend on consent. So if one is coerced into them and utters them, it is not invalidated by coercion, and they take effect upon the coerced (with fatha) only. And if it can be dissolved and depends on consent, like sale and the like, then it is confined to the direct agent here as well, which is dissolution like hire, except that it becomes corrupt due to lack of consent; so the sale is concluded as corrupt, and if the coerced ratifies it after coercion ceases, it becomes valid because the corrupting factor is removed by ratification. And all admissions are not valid because their validity depends on the existence of what is admitted; the indication of it [the admission] has demonstrated its absence, i.e., the non-existence of what is admitted, because he speaks to repel the sword from himself, not due to the existence of what is admitted. It is not permissible to make it a metaphor for something because he does not intend the metaphor while there is evidence of falsehood, which is coercion. And actions are of two types: one type is like words, so it is not appropriate for the coerced person to be a tool for another, such as eating... (interpretation: i.e., the admission) and its ruling is not established because it was issued under coercion. Analogy with jest is not valid, for the jester is pleased with effecting the cause, even if he is not pleased with the ruling; whereas in our case, the coerced is not pleased with the cause, rather he effects it under coercion, so it becomes null. Consider. (Al-Qamar) 'And does not depend' etc. such that it occurs by jest as well. (Al-Qamar) At-Tadbir: he says to his slave, for example: if I die, you are free. Az-Zihar: likening his wife or what is expressed by her or a common part of her to a body part to which it is unlawful for him to look from among his mahram relatives by lineage or suckling. Al-Ila: an oath preventing intercourse with the wife for the period of ila, which for a free woman is four months and for a slave woman two months. Al-Fay': returning from ila, which is the oath; verbal fay': he says, for example: I have returned to her. As in Al-Wiqayah and others. (Al-Qamar) The sale is concluded as corrupt. As for its conclusion, it is because it issues from its people in its place. As for its corruption, it is due to the loss of consent, which is a condition for validity. So much so that if the coerced ratifies after coercion ceases, it becomes valid due to the removal of the corrupting factor. (As-Sunbuli) 'All of them': i.e., whether they are of the type that can be dissolved or not, and whether by overwhelming coercion or otherwise. (Al-Qamar) And sexual intercourse, and fornication, are confined to the coerced person because eating with another's mouth is inconceivable, and likewise intercourse with another's organ is inconceivable. So if a person is coerced into eating during a fast, the fast of the eater is broken and the fast of the commander is not broken if he is fasting. Similarly, if he is coerced into eating another's property, the eater sins and the commander does not. However, they differed regarding liability: it is said that liability rests on the coerced, not the commander, even if the coerced is a tool for the commander in terms of destruction, because the benefit of eating accrues to him. And it is said: if he is coerced into eating his own property, if he is hungry, nothing is owed by the commander because his benefit returns to the eater; if he is full, its value is owed because his benefit did not return to the eater. And if he is coerced into eating another's property, liability rests on the coerced, whether he is hungry or full, because it is a kind of coercion to destroy his property, so liability is due. Likewise, if a person is coerced into intercourse, if it is with a woman other than his wife, the hadd punishment is upon him, and he suffers pain, and this act does not transfer to the commander according to what will follow. And if it is with his wife during a fast, i'tikaf, ihram, or menses, then this too should be confined to the agent, and he sins, and he must undertake what is required of making up and expiation, and liability upon his property, and I have not seen a narration that he may claim recourse against the coercer commander or not. (As-Sunbuli) 'If he is hungry' meaning the coerced eater. (Al-Qamar) 'Then the hadd is upon him': I said, and in some commentaries of Al-Husami: the hadd is not necessary upon either of them, but the 'aqr (dower equivalent) is due upon the one on whom intercourse is committed, and he does not have recourse against the one who compelled him because the benefit of intercourse accrued to him, and Allah knows best. (As-Sunbuli)
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage, from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh work, explains the effect of coercion (ikrah) on utterances and actions. It first distinguishes between utterances that cannot be dissolved and do not depend on consent — such as divorce, manumission, marriage, etc. — which remain valid despite coercion, because the speaker is considered a direct agent and coercion does not vitiate the legal cause. Conversely, utterances that can be dissolved and depend on consent, like sale, become corrupt (fasid) under coercion but are validated if the coerced person ratifies after coercion ends. Admissions (iqrar) are entirely invalid under coercion because they are presumptively false — the coerced person speaks only to avoid harm, not to affirm reality. The text then turns to actions, dividing them into two types: those like words (where the coerced cannot be a mere tool) and others. For physical acts like eating and intercourse, the coerced person is always the direct agent, so fasting may be broken, sin may be incurred, and liability for damages falls on him. The commentary (by al-Qamar, al-Sunbuli) nuances these rules, discussing disagreements about liability and whether the commander can be held responsible. The final principle states that all actions and words of the coerced person are considered valid because they issue from intellect and legal capacity, unless a changing factor (mughtayyar) like an exception or condition alters the ruling. This passage is typical of the Hanafi method of analyzing legal capacity (ahliyyah) and its impediments.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrāh): Compelling a person to do something against his will, either by threat of harm (mulji') or less.
الفساد (al-fasād): Corruption (voidability) of a contract due to missing a condition like consent; can become valid by ratification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling of a sale contract executed under coercion in Hanafi fiqh?
A) Batil (void) and cannot become valid.
B) Fasid (corrupt) and becomes valid if ratified after coercion ends.
C) Valid from the outset because sale does not require consent.
D) Suspended until the coercer approves.

Key Takeaway
A coerced sale is fasid but can become valid through ratification by the coerced party after coercion ends.

Coercion in Acts Not Possible as a Tool
Ruling on coerced eating and intercourse, with guarantee dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify acts where the coerced person is not considered a mere instrument (ālah) in Hanafi usul, and apply the rulings on liability (daman) and sin (ithm) to coerced eating and intercourse.

Core Idea
In acts like eating and intercourse, the coerced is not a tool—he bears liability and sin, not the commander.

Coercion often transfers legal effects to the coercer when the coerced is merely a tool. However, Hanafi usul recognizes that for certain acts—like eating or intercourse—the coerced person cannot be reduced to an instrument. This passage explores that boundary and its practical consequences for liability, sin, and validity of actions.

Guided Path
1
Define the Scope of Acts
Identify which acts are 'inconceivable' for the coerced to be a tool. The text mentions eating by another's mouth and intercourse with another's organ.
These acts are inherently tied to the doer's own body; they cannot be delegated.
2
Apply to Worship: Fasting
If a person is coerced to eat while fasting, his fast is invalidated, but the commander's fast (if he is fasting) is not affected.
The sin also falls on the coerced eater.
3
Apply to Property: Eating Another's Food
If coerced to eat another's property, the eater sins and is liable (daman). The commander is not liable, even if the coerced is a tool in destruction.
There is a dispute: some say if the eater is full, the commander should be liable because the benefit did not return to the eater.
4
Analyze the Dispute on Daman
Examine the two views: (1) coerced always liable, or (2) commander liable when coerced is full. Note the thamarah al-khilaf: difference in who pays compensation.
The text does not give a clear preference; both views are from Hanafi sources.
5
Extend to Intercourse
Apply the same principle to coerced intercourse. If with a non-wife, hadd penalty is on the doer. If with his wife in prohibited states (fasting, i'tikaf, ihram, menstruation), he must make up and expiate; liability is on his wealth.
The commander is not liable for hadd or kaffarah.
6
Introduce the Mughayyir Principle
Coerced actions are generally valid and attributed to the doer, unless a modifier (mughayyir) like an exception or condition changes the ruling, analogous to voluntary acts.
This principle serves as a general rule for all coerced acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوطء، والزنا، فيقتصر على المكره؛ لأن الأكل بفم الغير لا يتصوّر، وكذا الوطء بآلة الغير لا يتصوّر ، فإذا أكره الإنسان أن يأكل في الصوم يفسد صوم الأكل ولا يفسد صوم الأمر إن كان صائمًا، وكذا لو أكره أن يأكل مال غيره يأثم الأكل دون الأمر، أي الأمر ولكنهم اختلفوا في حق الضمان فقيل: يجب الضمان على المكره دون الآمر، وإن كان المكره يصلح آلة للآمر من حيث الإتلاف؛ لأن منفعة الأكل حصلت له، وقيل: لو أكره أي للمكره على أكل مال نفسه، فإن كان جائعا لا يجب على الآمر شيء؛ لأن منفعته رجعت إلى الأكل، وإن كان شبعان تجب عليه قيمته؛ لأن منفعته لم ترجعا إلى الآكل، ولو أكره الأمر أي الأكل من على أكل مال الغير يجب الضمان على المكره، سواء كان جائعا أو شبعان؛ لأنه قبيل الإكراه على إتلاف ماله فيجب الضمان وكذا إذا أكره إنسان أن يطأ، فإن كان مع غير امرأته، فيجب عليه الحد ويكون آلما، ولا ينتقل هذا الفعل إلى الأمر على ما الواطي سيأتي، وإن كان مع امرأته في الصوم، أو في الاعتكاف أو الإحرام، أو الحيض، فينبغي أن يكون هذا أيضًا مقتصراً على الفاعل، ويأثم هو، ويجب ما يجب من القضاء والكفارة، أي الواطي والضمان في ماله وما رأيت رواية على أنه يرجع به على المكره الآمر أم لا. الواطي بالضمان على المكره : إلا إذا غيره دليل مثل فعل الطائع أي كما أن فعل الطائع وقوله لا يبطل، بل يعتبر إلا إذا لحقه مغيّر من استثناء أو تعليق، فحينئذ لا يعتبر كما إذا قال لامرأته: أنت طالق" يقع الطلاق بعد التكلّم، إلا إذا لحقه دليل مغيّر فحينئذٍ لا يقع كالاستثناء والتعليق وكذا إذا شرب الخمر أو زنى يعتبر ذلك، ويقع عليه الحد، إلا إذا لحقه مانع ومغيّر كتحقق تلك الأفعال في دار الحرب أو تمكين الشبهة فيها، فحينئذ لا يعتبر، فكذلك جمع أفعال المكره وأقواله تعتبر وتصح لصدورها عن عقل وأهلية ،خطاب إلا عند وجود المغير، فحينئذ لا تصح ولا تعتبر . (السنبلي) فإن كان أي المكره الأكل جائعًا. (القمر) فيجب عليه الحد :قلت وقال في بعض شروح "الحسامي": لا يجب به الحدّ على واحد منهما، ويجب به العقر على المحمول، ولا يرجع به على الحامل؛ لأن منفعة الوطء حصلت له، والله تعالى أعلم. (السنبلي)
And intercourse and adultery, so it is confined to the coerced person because eating by another's mouth is inconceivable, likewise intercourse with another's organ is inconceivable. So if a person is coerced to eat while fasting, the fast of the eater is invalidated and the fast of the commander is not invalidated if he is fasting. Likewise, if he is coerced to eat another's property, the eater sins, not the commander. But they differed regarding liability: some said liability is on the coerced, not the commander, even if the coerced serves as a tool for the commander in terms of destruction, because the benefit of eating accrued to him. And it was said: if he is coerced to eat his own property, if he is hungry, nothing is owed by the commander, because the benefit returned to the eater, but if he is full, its value is owed because the benefit did not return to the eater. And if he is coerced to eat another's property, liability is on the coerced, whether hungry or full, because it is a case of coercion to destroy property, so liability is due. Likewise, if a person is coerced to have intercourse, if it is with other than his wife, the hadd penalty is obligatory upon him and he is sinful, and this act does not transfer to the commander as will come. And if it is with his wife while fasting, or in i'tikaf, or ihram, or menstruation, then this too should be confined to the doer. He sins, and he must make up and expiate, and the liability is on his wealth. I have not seen a narration that he can recover it from the coercer-commander. (al-Sanbuli) If the coerced eater is hungry. (al-Qamar) Hadd is obligatory upon him. I said: In some commentaries of al-Husami, it is said: Hadd is not obligatory on either of them, and 'aqr (compensation) is obligatory on the carried [i.e., the coerced], and he cannot recover it from the carrier, because the benefit of intercourse accrued to him. And Allah knows best. (al-Sanbuli)
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage from Noorul Anwar's commentary discusses coercion in acts where the coerced person cannot be considered a mere tool. The author explains that in eating and intercourse, the act is inherently tied to the doer; one cannot eat with another's mouth or have intercourse with another's organ. Therefore, the ruling (e.g., breaking fast, sin, liability) falls on the coerced actor, not on the commander. However, there is internal Hanafi disagreement on liability (daman) when the coerced person eats another's property: some hold the coerced liable because he directly consumed, others consider the commander liable if the coerced was full (benefit not returned). The passage also extends to intercourse: if coerced to commit zina, hadd is on the coerced. If coerced to have intercourse with his own wife in forbidden states, he must make up and expiate. The Sanbuli commentary adds that the coerced person's actions are generally valid unless a modifier (e.g., exception, condition) changes the ruling, analogizing to voluntary acts like divorce or drinking. This passage is a detailed exposition of the principle that the coerced is not an instrument in such acts.

Key Terms
آلة (ālah): A tool or instrument; the coerced person in certain acts is likened to a tool of the commander
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability or guarantee for property or compensation
مغير (mughayyir): A modifier that changes the legal effect of an action or speech, such as an exception or condition

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person is coerced to eat another's property while fasting, who is liable according to the majority view in this passage?
A) The commander alone
B) The coerced eater alone
C) Both are equally liable
D) No liability because of coercion

Key Takeaway
For acts where the coerced cannot be a mere instrument, the coerced alone bears the legal consequences, unless a modifier (mughayyir) changes the ruling.

Absolute Prohibitions Under Coercion: The Case of Zina
Four categories of prohibition that cannot be lifted under coercion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain why certain prohibitions like zina remain absolute under coercion, using the Hanafi usul classification.

Core Idea
Some prohibitions are irrevocable: coercion never lifts them.

Coercion often excuses actions that would otherwise be forbidden, but not all. This lesson explores a fundamental Hanafi usul principle: that certain harms, like the corruption of lineage, are so severe that no threat can justify them. We will focus on zina as the prime example, examining the reasoning and the underlying legal rationale.

Guided Path
1
Classify the prohibition
Identify the type of prohibition under discussion: 'a prohibition that is not removable and does not admit concession' (حرمة لا تنكشف ولا تدخلها رخصة). This is an absolute prohibition.
From the text: 'حرمة لا تنكشف'.
2
Identify the example
The text gives zina (unlawful intercourse) as the example of such an absolute prohibition. Note that even severe coercion (threat of death) does not make it permissible.
Zina is explicitly mentioned.
3
Locate the rationale
The rationale (علة) is 'corruption of the bed and loss of lineage' (فساد الفراش وضياع النسب). This is a permanent and irreparable harm, unlike loss of property or temporary injury.
This ʿilla justifies the absolute ruling.
4
Understand the effect on the coerced
The coerced person is considered the direct actor (فاعل) and bears sin; the coercer is like a tool. The coercer's threat corrupts the coerced's choice, but the nature of the act remains unchanged.
The text: 'يلحق بالآلة' (attached to the tool).
5
Note the consensus
The passage mentions that this ruling is by consensus (إجماع) according to some commentators, reinforcing its strength in Hanafi usul.
From al-Sanbuli: 'هذا الحكم بالإجماع'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحرمات أنواع: حرمة لا تنكشف ولا تدخلها رخصة كالزنا بالمرأة، فإنه لا يحلّ بعذر الإكراه قط؛ إذ فيه فساد الفراش وضياع النسب؛ لأن ولد الزنا هالك حكمًا؛ إذ لا تحب أربعة على الأم نفقته، ولا يجب على الزاني تأديبه وإنفاقه، فهو داخل في الإكراه الحظر على المكره ويخرج المكره بالفتح من البين، ويلحق بالآلة لفساد اختياره بالإكراه الكامل؛ إذ هو ملجأ في هذا الفعل، والإنسان مجبول على حبّ الحياة، فلما هدّد بالقتل بأن قال المكره بالكسر : "اقتل فلانًا وأتلف ماله وإلا لأقتلنّك" وطلب لنفسه مخلصاً عن الهلاك بالإقدام على القتل أو تلف الأموال وإن كان حرامًا فسد اختياره بهذا الوجه. (السنبلي) عند أبي حنيفة ليه قلت: قال بعض الشارحين للحسامي": إن هذا الحكم بالإجماع، والله تعالى أعلم، ولعل التحقيق يحصل بعد الرجوع إلى الفتاوى (السنبلي) دارئة أي دافعة له أي للقصاص عنهما، أي عن الآمر والمأمور. (القمر) وضياع النسب: فكأنه قتل الولد؛ لأن إلخ. (القمر) الإكراه الحظر : أي في العمل بالإكراه الذي كان حظراً. (القمر)
And prohibitions are of types: a prohibition that is not removable and does not admit concession, like zina with a woman, for it is never made permissible by the excuse of coercion, because it involves corruption of the bed and loss of lineage, for the child of zina is considered lost (hālik) legally, since it does not become obligatory upon the mother his maintenance, nor is it obligatory upon the fornicator to discipline and spend on him. So it falls under coercion of prohibition (ikrāh al-ḥaẓr) upon the coerced, and the coercer (with fatḥ) is excluded and attached to the tool, due to the corruption of his choice by complete coercion, for he is compelled in this act. And man is naturally inclined to love life, so when he is threatened with death by the coercer saying, 'Kill so-and-so and destroy his property, or else I will kill you,' and he seeks for himself an escape from destruction by proceeding to kill or destroy property, even if it is forbidden, his choice is corrupted in this way. (al-Sanbuli) According to Abu Hanifa. I said: Some commentators of al-Husami said that this ruling is by consensus, and Allah knows best, and perhaps verification will be obtained after referring to fatwas. (al-Sanbuli) 'Dāri'ah' meaning repelling, i.e., repelling qisas from both, i.e., the commander and the commanded. (al-Qamar) 'Loss of lineage': it is as if he killed the child, because etc. (al-Qamar) 'Ikrah al-ḥaẓr': i.e., in the act by coercion that was a prohibition. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage categorizes prohibitions (ḥurumāt) in the context of coercion into four types, though only the first type is detailed here. The first type is a prohibition that cannot be lifted under any circumstances, such as zina (adultery/fornication). Even under the most severe coercion (threat of death), zina remains absolutely forbidden. The rationale is that zina causes corruption of lineage (i.e., illegitimate children) and destroys the family structure. The coerced person is considered the direct actor and bears sin; the coercer is likened to a tool. The passage also discusses how coercion that threatens life corrupts the coerced's choice, pushing him to commit forbidden acts like killing. The Sanbuli commentary adds that the ruling on zina's impermissibility under coercion is by consensus (ijmā'), though some refer to fatwa collections for verification. The Qamar commentary explains terms like 'dāri'ah' (repelling doubt) and 'ikrāh al-ḥaẓr' (coercion of prohibition). This classification helps determine which acts are excused by coercion and which are not.

Key Terms
زنا (zinā): Unlawful sexual intercourse; one of the prohibitions that cannot be excused even under coercion
فساد الفراش (fasād al-firāsh): Corruption of the marital bed; refers to mixing of lineages and illegitimacy
إكراه الحظر (ikrāh al-ḥaẓr): Coercion to commit a prohibited act; this category includes zina and similar acts

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is zina never permissible even under coercion?
A) Because the coerced person has no choice
B) Because it causes corruption of lineage and family
C) Because it is a major sin
D) Because the coercer is the real actor

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, prohibitions involving permanent harm (like loss of lineage) are absolute and cannot be lifted by coercion.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
10 min
Rukhsah vs Ibahah Under Coercion in Hanafi Usul
Explains the third and fourth categories of prohibited acts under coercion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between rukhsah (concession) and ibahah (permissibility) in cases of coercion according to Hanafi usul, and apply the distinction to determine the legal status of an action under overwhelming coercion.

Core Idea
Under coercion, prohibition remains; only sin is removed — that's rukhsah, not ibahah.

In Hanafi usul, coercion does not render the prohibited permissible; it merely suspends punishment. This lesson unpacks the critical distinction between rukhsah (where prohibition remains but sin is lifted) and ibahah (where prohibition is entirely removed). Understanding this difference is key to correctly classifying actions under duress and avoiding the common error of treating all coerced acts as permissible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of coercion
Determine if the coercion is overwhelming (ikrah mulji') — immediate threat of death or severe bodily harm. Only this triggers rukhsah.
If coercion is non-overwhelming, the prohibition remains fully, and the act is sinful.
2
Classify the act under coercion
The coerced act is treated as a rukhsah (concession), not ibahah (permissibility). The prohibition persists, but the sin is removed to avoid greater harm.
Some scholars debate whether it falls under obligation or concession; see al_mukhtalaf_fih.
3
Apply to property consumption
If the coerced act involves consuming another's property, the act is a rukhsah: it is allowed under duress, but compensation (daman) must be paid afterward.
Compensation is required because the prohibition (hurmat) is not lifted.
4
Understand the consequence of patience
If the coerced person endures patiently and is killed, they become a martyr because they refused to violate the prohibition despite coercion.
This contrasts with ibahah, where no reward is given for refraining since the act is permitted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أنواع حرمات المكره به ويترخص فيه لدفع الشر، ويعامل معاملة المباح، فإذا أكره بالإكراه الملجئ جاز له أن تناول مال الغير في ذلك خاصة يفعل ذلك ثم يضمن قيمته بعد زوال الإكراه لبقاء عصمته، فهو أيضا داخل في قسم أي تناول مال الغير أي الفاعل المكره مال الغير مال الغير الرخصة. ولم يتعرّض لقسم الإباحة لِمَا قدّمنا أنها إمّا داخلة في الفرض أو في الرخصة. ولهذا، أي ولأجل أن الحرمة لم تسقط في القسم الثالث والرابع. إذا صبر في هذين القسمين حتى قتل صار شهيدًا؛ لأنه يكون باذلا نفسه لإعزاز دين الله تعالى ولإقامة الشرع. اللهم أدخلني في زمرة الشهداء، واسلكني في عدة السعداء يوما لا ينفع مال ولا بنون، ولا ينجي بأس ولا حصون بحرمة نبينا وشفيعنا محمد وعلى آله وأصحابه وأهل بيته وأزواجه وذرياته وسلّم. ويترخص فيه: فالقسم الثالث والرابع للحرمة مرخص فيها عند الإكراه الكامل لا مباح؛ لأن حرمتها باقية على حالهما، وإنما رخص للمكره في الإكراه الكامل دفعًا للحرج، ولهذا لو صبر المكره حتى قتل كان شهيدًا ومأجورًا إن شاء الله تعالى بخلاف المباح حيث لا يبقى الحرمة فيها ولا يؤجر المكره في امتناعه عنه، بل يأثم. (السنبلي) لقسم الإباحة والفرق بين الرخصة والإباحة هو أن في المباح ترتفع الحرمة وفي الرخصة لا ترتفع، بل يرفع الإثم فقط، قال بعض الأصوليين والأولى عدم ذكر الإباحة؛ لأنها إن كان مع الإثم في الصبر فهي الفرض وإلا فهي الرخصة، فالحاصل أنها داخل في الفرض أو الرخصة، ولذلك قال الشارح له : لما قدمنا أنها إما داخلة في الفرض أو في الرخصة (السنبلي)
Explanation of the types of prohibitions of that which one is coerced into. And a concession is granted for it to repel harm, and it is treated as permissible. So if someone is coerced with overwhelming coercion, it is permissible for him to consume the property of another in that particular case. He does that, then he guarantees its value after the coercion ceases due to the remaining inviolability of his person (ismah). So it is also included in the category of concession (rukhsah). And he did not mention the category of permissibility (ibahah) because we have stated that it is either included in obligation (fard) or in concession. And for this reason, i.e., because the prohibition is not dropped in the third and fourth categories. If one endures in these two categories until he is killed, he becomes a martyr because he sacrifices himself for the glorification of Allah's religion and for establishing the Shari'ah. ... And the concession is granted: the third and fourth categories of prohibition are made a concession under complete coercion, not permissible; because their prohibition remains as it is, but a concession is given to the coerced in complete coercion to remove hardship. So if the coerced endures until killed, he is a martyr and rewarded, unlike the permissible where the prohibition does not remain and the coerced is not rewarded for refraining from it, rather he sins. (al-Sunbuli) And the difference between concession and permissibility is that in permissibility the prohibition is lifted, while in concession it is not lifted, rather only the sin is removed. Some usul scholars said it is better not to mention permissibility because if there is sin in patience then it is obligation, otherwise it is concession. So the conclusion is that it is included in either obligation or concession. Therefore the commentator said: 'Because we have stated that it is either included in obligation or in concession.' (al-Sunbuli)
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Anwar explains the legal status of prohibited acts under coercion, focusing on the third and fourth categories of prohibition. The author states that when a person is coerced with overwhelming coercion (ikrah mulji'), he may perform the prohibited act as a concession (rukhsah) to repel harm, but he must compensate if property is taken. The prohibition itself is not lifted; only the sin is removed. This distinguishes rukhsah from ibahah (permissibility), where the prohibition is entirely lifted. The commentator (sub-commentator) emphasizes that patience and refusal even under coercion leads to martyrdom, whereas if the act were permissible, no reward would be earned for refraining. Al-Sunbuli clarifies that some scholars avoid categorizing as ibahah because it either falls under obligation (if patience is sinful) or concession. The Hanafi position is clear: coercion does not render the prohibited permissible; it only suspends punishment.

Key Terms
الرخصة (al-rukhsah): A concession where the prohibition remains but the sin is removed due to necessity
الإباحة (al-ibahah): Permissibility where the prohibition is entirely lifted
الإكراه الملجئ (al-ikrah al-mulji'): Overwhelming coercion that compels an action under threat of severe harm

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul presented, what is the status of consuming someone else's property under overwhelming coercion (ikrah mulji')?
A) It becomes permissible (mubah) and no compensation is due.
B) It is a concession (rukhsah) but compensation must be paid afterward.
C) It remains strictly prohibited, and the coerced sins regardless.
D) It is obligatory (fard) because of the coercion.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, overwhelming coercion transforms a prohibited act into a rukhsah (concession) where the prohibition remains but sin is removed; it does not become ibahah (permissible).
